<h1 id="firstHeading" class="firstHeading" lang="en">Family planning</h1>			<div id="bodyContent" class="mw-body-content">
				<div id="siteSub" class="noprint">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>				<div id="contentSub"></div>
				<div id="jump-to-nav"></div>				<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#mw-head">Jump to navigation</a>
				<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#p-search">Jump to search</a>
				<div id="mw-content-text" lang="en" dir="ltr" class="mw-content-ltr"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Ortho_tricyclen.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fe/Ortho_tricyclen.jpg/220px-Ortho_tricyclen.jpg" width="220" height="185" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fe/Ortho_tricyclen.jpg/330px-Ortho_tricyclen.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fe/Ortho_tricyclen.jpg/440px-Ortho_tricyclen.jpg 2x" data-file-width="952" data-file-height="800" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Ortho_tricyclen.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Combined_oral_contraceptive" class="mw-redirect" title="Combined oral contraceptive">Combined oral contraceptives</a>. Introduced in 1960, "the Pill" has played an instrumental role in family planning for decades.</div></div></div>
<p><b>Family planning</b> services are defined as "educational, comprehensive medical or social activities which enable individuals, including minors, to determine freely the number and spacing of their children and to select the means by which this may be achieved".<sup id="cite_ref-USDOH-FPSvcs_1-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-USDOH-FPSvcs-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup>  Family planning may involve consideration of the number of children a woman wishes to have, including the choice to have no children, as well as the age at which she wishes to have them. These matters are influenced by external factors such as marital situation, career considerations, financial position, any disabilities that may affect their ability to have children and raise them, besides many other considerations. If sexually active, family planning may involve the use of <a href="/wiki/Birth_control" title="Birth control">contraception</a><sup id="cite_ref-WHOFP_2-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WHOFP-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-UKNHS_3-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-UKNHS-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup> and other techniques to control the timing of <a href="/wiki/Reproduction" title="Reproduction">reproduction</a>. Other techniques commonly used include <a href="/wiki/Sex_education" title="Sex education">sexuality education</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-UKNHS_3-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-UKNHS-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-USDOH-FPSvcs_1-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-USDOH-FPSvcs-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup>  prevention and management of <a href="/wiki/Sexually_transmitted_infection" title="Sexually transmitted infection">sexually transmitted infections</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-UKNHS_3-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-UKNHS-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Pre-conception_counseling" title="Pre-conception counseling">pre-conception counseling</a><sup id="cite_ref-UKNHS_3-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-UKNHS-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup>  and <a href="/wiki/Pregnancy#Management" title="Pregnancy">management</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Infertility" title="Infertility">infertility</a> management.<sup id="cite_ref-WHOFP_2-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WHOFP-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup> Family planning as defined by the United Nations and the World Health Organization encompasses services leading up to conception and does not promote abortion as a family planning method,<sup id="cite_ref-Family_planning_4-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Family_planning-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup> although levels of contraceptive use reduces the need for abortion.<sup id="cite_ref-pmid24670784_5-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pmid24670784-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Family planning is sometimes used as a synonym or <a href="/wiki/Euphemism" title="Euphemism">euphemism</a> for access to and the use of <a href="/wiki/Contraception" class="mw-redirect" title="Contraception">contraception</a>. However, it often involves methods and practices in addition to contraception. Additionally, there are many who might wish to use contraception but are not, necessarily, planning a family (e.g., unmarried adolescents, young married couples delaying childbearing while building a career); family planning has become a catch-all phrase for much of the work undertaken in this realm. Contemporary notions of family planning, however, tend to place a woman and her childbearing decisions at the center of the discussion, as notions of women's empowerment and reproductive autonomy have gained traction in many parts of the world. It is most usually applied to a <a href="/wiki/Female" title="Female">female</a>-<a href="/wiki/Male" title="Male">male</a> couple who wish to limit the number of children they have and/or to control the timing of <a href="/wiki/Pregnancy" title="Pregnancy">pregnancy</a> (also known as <i><a href="/wiki/Birth_spacing" title="Birth spacing">spacing children</a></i>).
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Purposes">Purposes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Family_planning&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Purposes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>In 2006, the US <a href="/wiki/Centers_for_Disease_Control" class="mw-redirect" title="Centers for Disease Control">Centers for Disease Control</a> (CDC) issued a recommendation, encouraging men and women to formulate a reproductive life plan, to help them in avoiding <a href="/wiki/Unintended_pregnancies" class="mw-redirect" title="Unintended pregnancies">unintended pregnancies</a> and to improve the health of women and reduce adverse pregnancy outcomes.<sup id="cite_ref-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Raising a child requires significant amounts of resources: time,<sup id="cite_ref-USDAChildExpense_7-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-USDAChildExpense-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> social, financial,<sup id="cite_ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup> and environmental<sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup>. Planning can help assure that resources are available. The purpose of family planning is to make sure that any couple, man, or woman who has a child has the resources that are needed in order to complete this goal.<sup id="cite_ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Accuracy_dispute#Disputed_statement" title="Wikipedia:Accuracy dispute"><span title="The material near this tag is possibly inaccurate or nonfactual. (March 2012)">dubious</span></a>&#32;<span class="metadata"> &#8211; <a href="/wiki/Talk:Family_planning#Dubious" title="Talk:Family planning">discuss</a></span></i>&#93;</sup>  With these resources a couple, man or women can explore the options of natural birth, <a href="/wiki/Surrogacy" title="Surrogacy">surrogacy</a>, <a href="/wiki/Artificial_insemination" title="Artificial insemination">artificial insemination</a>, or <a href="/wiki/Adoption" title="Adoption">adoption</a>. In the other case, if the person does not wish to have a child at the specific time, they can investigate the resources that are needed to prevent pregnancy, such as birth control, contraceptives, or physical protection and prevention.
</p><p>There is no clear social impact case for or against conceiving a child. Individually, for most people,<sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup> bearing a child or not has no measurable impact on person well-being. A review of the economic literature on life satisfaction shows that certain groups of people are much happier without children:
</p>
<ul><li>Single parents</li>
<li>Fathers who both work and raise the children equally.</li>
<li>Singles</li>
<li>The divorced</li>
<li>The poor</li>
<li>Those whose children are older than 3</li>
<li>Those whose children are sick<sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup></li></ul>
<p>However, both adoptees and the adopters report that they are happier after adoption.<sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup> Adoption may also insure against costs of prenatal or childhood disability which can be anticipated with prenatal screening or with reference to parental risk factors. For instance, older fathers<sup id="cite_ref-pmid28088314_14-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pmid28088314-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup> and/or <a href="/wiki/Advanced_maternal_age" title="Advanced maternal age">Advanced maternal age</a> increase the risk of numerous health issues in their offspring, including autism and schizophrenia.<a href="/w/index.php?title=Template:Sanchez,_2018&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Template:Sanchez, 2018 (page does not exist)">Template:Sanchez, 2018</a>
</p><p><br />
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Resources">Resources</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Family_planning&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Resources">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>When women can pursue additional education and paid employment, families can invest more in each child. Children with fewer siblings tend to stay in school longer than those with many siblings. Leaving school in order to have children has long-term implications for the future of these girls, as well as the human capital of their families and communities. Family planning slows unsustainable population growth which drains resources from the environment, and national and regional development efforts.<sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Family_planning/Contraception_16-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Family_planning/Contraception-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Health">Health</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Family_planning&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Health">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Maternal_health" title="Maternal health">Maternal health</a>, <a href="/wiki/Maternal_death" title="Maternal death">Maternal death</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Teenage_pregnancy" title="Teenage pregnancy">teenage pregnancy</a></div>
<p>The WHO states about maternal health that:
</p>
<dl><dd>"Maternal health refers to the health of women during pregnancy, childbirth and the postpartum period. While motherhood is often a positive and fulfilling experience, for too many women it is associated with suffering, ill-health and even death."</dd></dl>
<p>About 99% of <a href="/wiki/Maternal_deaths" class="mw-redirect" title="Maternal deaths">maternal deaths</a> occur in <a href="/wiki/Developing_country" title="Developing country">less developed countries</a>; less than one half occur in sub-Saharan Africa and almost a third in South Asia.<sup id="cite_ref-who.int_17-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-who.int-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Both early and late motherhood have increased risks. Young teenagers face a higher risk of complications and death as a result of pregnancy.<sup id="cite_ref-who.int_17-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-who.int-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup> Waiting until the mother is at least 18 years old before trying to have children improves maternal and child health.<sup id="cite_ref-USAIDHTSP_18-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-USAIDHTSP-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Also, if additional children are desired after a child is born, it is healthier for the mother and the child to wait at least 2 years after the previous birth before attempting to conceive (but not more than 5 years).<sup id="cite_ref-USAIDHTSP_18-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-USAIDHTSP-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup> After a <a href="/wiki/Miscarriage" title="Miscarriage">miscarriage</a> or abortion, it is healthier to wait at least 6 months.<sup id="cite_ref-USAIDHTSP_18-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-USAIDHTSP-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Joselyne When planning a family, women should be aware that reproductive risks increase with the age of the woman. Like older men, older women have a higher chance of having a child with <a href="/wiki/Autism" title="Autism">autism</a> or <a href="/wiki/Down_syndrome" title="Down syndrome">Down syndrome</a>, the chances of having <a href="/wiki/Multiple_birth" title="Multiple birth">multiple births</a> increases, which cause further late-pregnancy risks, they have an increased chance of developing <a href="/wiki/Gestational_diabetes" title="Gestational diabetes">gestational diabetes</a>, the need for a <a href="/wiki/Caesarian_section" class="mw-redirect" title="Caesarian section">Caesarian section</a> is greater, older women's bodies are not as well-suited for delivering a baby.  The risk of prolonged labor is higher. Older mothers have a higher risk of a long labor, putting the baby in distress.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Familiy_Planning_Ethiopia_(bad_effects).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/74/Familiy_Planning_Ethiopia_%28bad_effects%29.jpg/220px-Familiy_Planning_Ethiopia_%28bad_effects%29.jpg" width="220" height="180" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/74/Familiy_Planning_Ethiopia_%28bad_effects%29.jpg/330px-Familiy_Planning_Ethiopia_%28bad_effects%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/74/Familiy_Planning_Ethiopia_%28bad_effects%29.jpg/440px-Familiy_Planning_Ethiopia_%28bad_effects%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2780" data-file-height="2276" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Familiy_Planning_Ethiopia_(bad_effects).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Placard showing negative effects of lack of family planning and having too many <a href="/wiki/Child" title="Child">children</a> and <a href="/wiki/Infant" title="Infant">infants</a> (<a href="/wiki/Ethiopia" title="Ethiopia">Ethiopia</a>)</div></div></div>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Modern_methods">Modern methods</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Family_planning&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Modern methods">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Modern methods of family planning include birth control, <a href="/wiki/Assisted_reproductive_technology" title="Assisted reproductive technology">assisted reproductive technology</a> and family planning programs.
</p><p>In regard to the use of modern methods of contraception, <a href="/wiki/The_United_Nations_Population_Fund" class="mw-redirect" title="The United Nations Population Fund">The United Nations Population Fund</a> (UNFPA) says that, “Contraceptives prevent unintended pregnancies, reduce the number of abortions, and lower the incidence of death and disability related to complications of pregnancy and childbirth.” <sup id="cite_ref-unfpa1_19-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-unfpa1-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup> UNFPA states that, “If all women with an unmet need for contraceptives were able to use modern methods, an additional 24 million abortions (14 million of which would be unsafe), 6 million miscarriages, 70,000 maternal deaths and 500,000 infant deaths would be prevented.” <sup id="cite_ref-unfpa1_19-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-unfpa1-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In cases where couples may not want to have children just yet, family planning programs help a lot. Federal family planning programs reduced childbearing among poor women by as much as 29 percent, according to a <a href="/wiki/University_of_Michigan" title="University of Michigan">University of Michigan</a> study.<sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Adoption" title="Adoption">Adoption</a> is another option used to build a family. There are seven steps that one must make towards adoption. You must decide to pursue an adoption, apply to adopt, complete an adoption home study, get approved to adopt, be matched with a child, receive an adoptive placement, and then legalize the adoption.<sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Contraception">Contraception</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Family_planning&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Contraception">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Birth_control" title="Birth control">Birth control</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Family_planning_Ethiopia_(good_effects).JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/08/Family_planning_Ethiopia_%28good_effects%29.JPG/220px-Family_planning_Ethiopia_%28good_effects%29.JPG" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/08/Family_planning_Ethiopia_%28good_effects%29.JPG/330px-Family_planning_Ethiopia_%28good_effects%29.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/08/Family_planning_Ethiopia_%28good_effects%29.JPG/440px-Family_planning_Ethiopia_%28good_effects%29.JPG 2x" data-file-width="3456" data-file-height="2592" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Family_planning_Ethiopia_(good_effects).JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Placard showing positive effects of family planning (Ethiopia)</div></div></div>
<p>A number of contraceptive methods are available to prevent <a href="/wiki/Unwanted_pregnancy" class="mw-redirect" title="Unwanted pregnancy">unwanted pregnancy</a>. There are natural methods and various chemical-based methods, <a href="/wiki/Comparison_of_contraceptive_methods" class="mw-redirect" title="Comparison of contraceptive methods">each with particular advantages and disadvantages</a>. Behavioral methods to avoid <a href="/wiki/Pregnancy" title="Pregnancy">pregnancy</a> that involve vaginal intercourse include the <a href="/wiki/Coitus_interruptus" title="Coitus interruptus">withdrawal</a> and <a href="/wiki/Calendar-based_methods" class="mw-redirect" title="Calendar-based methods">calendar-based methods</a>, which have little upfront cost and are readily available. <a href="/wiki/Long-acting_reversible_contraceptive" class="mw-redirect" title="Long-acting reversible contraceptive">Long-acting reversible contraceptive</a> methods, such as <a href="/wiki/Intrauterine_device" title="Intrauterine device">intrauterine device</a> (IUD) and implant are highly effective and convenient, requiring little user action, but do come with risks. When cost of failure is included, IUDs and <a href="/wiki/Vasectomy" title="Vasectomy">vasectomy</a> are much less costly than other methods. In addition to providing birth control, <a href="/wiki/Condom" title="Condom">male</a> and/or <a href="/wiki/Female_condom" title="Female condom">female condoms</a> protect against <a href="/wiki/Sexually_transmitted_diseases" class="mw-redirect" title="Sexually transmitted diseases">sexually transmitted diseases</a> (STD). Condoms may be used alone, or in addition to other methods, as backup or to prevent STD. Surgical methods (<a href="/wiki/Tubal_ligation" title="Tubal ligation">tubal ligation</a>, <a href="/wiki/Vasectomy" title="Vasectomy">vasectomy</a>) provide long-term contraception for those who have completed their families.<sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Assisted_reproductive_technology">Assisted reproductive technology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Family_planning&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Assisted reproductive technology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Assisted_reproductive_technology" title="Assisted reproductive technology">Assisted reproductive technology</a></div>
<p>When, for any reason, a woman is unable to conceive by natural means, she may seek assisted conception. For example, some families or women seek assistance through <a href="/wiki/Surrogacy" title="Surrogacy">surrogacy</a>, in which a woman agrees to become pregnant and deliver a child for another couple or person.
</p><p>There are two types of surrogacy: traditional and <a href="/wiki/Gestational" class="mw-redirect" title="Gestational">gestational</a>. In traditional surrogacy, the surrogate uses her own eggs <i>and</i> carries the child for her intended parents. This procedure is done in a doctor's office through IUI. This type of surrogacy obviously includes a genetic connection between the surrogate and the child. Legally, the surrogate will have to disclaim any interest in the child to complete the transfer to the intended parents. A gestational surrogacy occurs when the intended mother's or a donor egg is fertilized outside the body and then the embryos are transferred into the uterus. The woman who carries the child is often referred to as a gestational carrier. The legal steps to confirm parentage with the intended parents are generally easier than in a traditional because there is no genetic connection between child and carrier.<sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Sperm_donation" title="Sperm donation">Sperm donation</a> is another form of assisted conception. It involves donated sperm being used to fertilise a woman's ova by <a href="/wiki/Artificial_insemination" title="Artificial insemination">artificial insemination</a> (either by <a href="/wiki/Intracervical_insemination" class="mw-redirect" title="Intracervical insemination">intracervical insemination</a> or <a href="/wiki/Intrauterine_insemination" class="mw-redirect" title="Intrauterine insemination">intrauterine insemination</a>) and less commonly by invitro fertilization (<a href="/wiki/IVF" class="mw-redirect" title="IVF">IVF</a>), but insemination may also be achieved by a donor having <a href="/wiki/Sexual_intercourse" title="Sexual intercourse">sexual intercourse</a> with a woman for the purpose of achieving conception. This method is known as natural <a href="/wiki/Insemination" title="Insemination">insemination</a> (NI).<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (April 2018)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>
</p><p>Mapping of a woman's <a href="/wiki/Ovarian_reserve" title="Ovarian reserve">ovarian reserve</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ovarian_follicle" title="Ovarian follicle">follicular</a> dynamics and associated <a href="/wiki/Female_reproductive_biomarkers" class="mw-redirect" title="Female reproductive biomarkers">biomarkers</a> can give an individual prognosis about future chances of pregnancy, facilitating an informed choice of when to have children.<sup id="cite_ref-pmid23103636_24-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pmid23103636-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Finances">Finances</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Family_planning&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Finances">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Family_economics" title="Family economics">Family economics</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cost_of_raising_a_child" title="Cost of raising a child">Cost of raising a child</a></div>
<p>Family planning is among the most cost-effective of all health interventions.<sup id="cite_ref-pmid20570955_25-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pmid20570955-25">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup>  "The cost savings stem from a reduction in unintended pregnancy, as well as a reduction in transmission of sexually transmitted infections, including HIV".<sup id="cite_ref-pmid20570955_25-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pmid20570955-25">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Childbirth and prenatal health care cost averaged $7,090 for normal delivery in the United States in 1996.<sup id="cite_ref-pmid9691358_26-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pmid9691358-26">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/U.S._Department_of_Agriculture" class="mw-redirect" title="U.S. Department of Agriculture">U.S. Department of Agriculture</a> estimates that for a child born in 2007, a U.S. family will spend an average of $11,000 to $23,000 per year for the first 17 years of child's life.<sup id="cite_ref-USDAChildExpense_7-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-USDAChildExpense-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup>  (Total inflation-adjusted estimated expenditure: $196,000 to $393,000, depending on household income.)<sup id="cite_ref-USDAChildExpense_7-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-USDAChildExpense-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> Breaks down cost by age, type of expense, region of country.  Adjustments for number of children (one child — spend 24% more, 3 or more spend less on each child.)
</p><p>Investing in family planning has clear economic benefits and can also help countries to achieve their “demographic dividend,” which means that countries productivity is able to increase when there are more people in the workforce and less dependents.<sup id="cite_ref-unfpa1_19-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-unfpa1-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup> UNFPA says that, “For every dollar invested in contraception, the cost of pregnancy-related care is reduced by $1.47.”<sup id="cite_ref-unfpa1_19-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-unfpa1-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>UNFPA states that,
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r856303468">.mw-parser-output .templatequote{overflow:hidden;margin:1em 0;padding:0 40px}.mw-parser-output .templatequote .templatequotecite{line-height:1.5em;text-align:left;padding-left:1.6em;margin-top:0}</style><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>The lifetime opportunity cost related to adolescent pregnancy – a measure of the annual income a young mother misses out on over her lifetime – ranges from 1 per cent of annual gross domestic product in a large country such as China to 30 per cent of annual GDP in a small economy such as Uganda. If adolescent girls in Brazil and India were able to wait until their early twenties to have children, the increased economic productivity would equal more than $3.5 billion and $7.7 billion, respectively.<sup id="cite_ref-unfpa1_19-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-unfpa1-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup>
</p></blockquote>
<p>In the Copenhagen Consensus produced by Nobel laureates in collaboration with the UN, universal access to contraception ranks as the third highest policy initiative in social, economic, and environmental benefits for every dollar spent.<sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup> Providing universal access to sexual and reproductive health services and eliminating the unmet need for contraception will result in 640,000 fewer newborn deaths, 150,000 fewer maternal deaths and 600,000 fewer children who lose their mother. At the same time, societies will experience fewer dependents and more women in the workforce, driving faster economic growth. The costs of universal access to contraceptives will be about $3.6 billion/year, but the benefits will be more than $400 billion annually and cut maternal deaths by 150,000.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Fertility_Awareness">Fertility Awareness</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Family_planning&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Fertility Awareness">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Fertility_awareness" title="Fertility awareness">Fertility awareness</a></div>
<p>Fertility awareness refers to a set of practices used to determine the <a href="/wiki/Fertility" title="Fertility">fertile</a> and infertile phases of a woman's <a href="/wiki/Menstrual_cycle" title="Menstrual cycle">menstrual cycle</a>. Fertility awareness methods may be used to avoid pregnancy, to <a href="/wiki/Fertilisation" title="Fertilisation">achieve pregnancy</a>, or as a way to monitor <a href="/wiki/Female_reproductive_system_(human)" class="mw-redirect" title="Female reproductive system (human)">gynecological</a> health. Methods of identifying infertile days have been known since antiquity, but scientific knowledge gained during the past century has increased the number and variety of methods. Various methods can be used and the Symptothermal method has achieved a success rates over 99% if used properly.<sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>These methods are used for various reasons: There are no drug-related side effects,<sup id="cite_ref-Manhart_et_al_2013_29-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Manhart_et_al_2013-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup> it is free to use and only has a small upfront cost, it works both ways, or for religious reasons (the <a href="/wiki/Catholic_Church" title="Catholic Church">Catholic Church</a> promotes this as the only acceptable form of family planning calling it <a href="/wiki/Natural_Family_Planning" class="mw-redirect" title="Natural Family Planning">Natural Family Planning</a>). Its disadvantages are that either abstinence or backup method is required on fertile days, typical use is often less effective than other methods,<sup id="cite_ref-pmid19041435_30-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pmid19041435-30">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup> and it does not protect against <a href="/wiki/Sexually_transmitted_disease" class="mw-redirect" title="Sexually transmitted disease">sexually transmitted disease</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-uihc_31-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-uihc-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Media_campaign">Media campaign</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Family_planning&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Media campaign">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Recent research based on nationally representative surveys supports a strong association between family planning mass media campaigns and contraceptive use, even after social and demographic variables are controlled for. The 1989 Kenya Demographic and Health Survey found half of the women who recalled hearing or seeing family planning messages in radio, print, and television consequently used contraception, compared with 14% who did not recall family planning messages in the media, even after age, residence and socioeconomic status were taken into account.<sup id="cite_ref-Guttmacher_Institute_32-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Guttmacher_Institute-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The Health Education Division of the Ministry of Health conducted the Tanzanian Family Planning Communication Project from January 1991 through December 1994, a project funded by the U.S. Agency for International Development (USAID).<sup id="cite_ref-Guttmacher_Institute_32-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Guttmacher_Institute-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup> The program intended to educate both men and men of reproductive age about modern contraception methods. The major media channels and products included radio spots, radio series drama, Green Star logo promotional activities (identifies sites where family planning services are available), posters, leaflets, newspapers, and audio cassettes. In conjunction with other non-project interventions sponsored by other Tanzanian and international agencies from 1992–1994, contraception use among women ages 15–49 increased from 5.9% to 11.3%. The total fertility rate dropped from 6.3 lifetime births per individual in 1991–1992 to 5.8 in 1994.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Providers">Providers</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Family_planning&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Providers">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Direct_government_support">Direct government support</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Family_planning&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Direct government support">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Direct government support for family planning includes providing family planning education and supplies through government-run facilities such as hospitals, clinics, health posts and health centers and through government fieldworkers.<sup id="cite_ref-AutoW0-18_33-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AutoW0-18-33">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In 2013, 160 out of 197 governments provided direct support for family planning. Twenty countries only provided indirect support through private sector or NGOs. Seventeen governments did not support family planning. Direct government support has continued to increase in developing countries from 82% in 1996 to 93% in 2013, but is declining in developed countries from 58% in 1976 to 45% in 2013. Ninety-seven percent of Latin America and the Caribbean, 96% of Africa, and 94% of Oceania governments provided direct support for family planning. In Europe, only 45% of governments directly support family planning. Out of 172 countries with available data in 2012, 152 countries had implemented realistic measures to increase women’s access to family planning methods from 2009–2014. This included 95% of developing nations and 65% of developed nations.<sup id="cite_ref-AutoW0-18_33-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AutoW0-18-33">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Private_sector">Private sector</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Family_planning&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Private sector">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The private sector includes nongovernmental and faith-based organizations who typically provide free or subsidized services to for-profit medical providers, pharmacies and drug shops. The private sector accounts for approximately two-fifths of contraceptive suppliers worldwide. Private organizations are able to provide sustainable markets for contraceptive services through social marketing, social franchising, and pharmacies.<sup id="cite_ref-AutoW0-19_34-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AutoW0-19-34">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Social marketing employs marketing techniques to achieve behavioral change while making contraceptives available. By utilizing private providers, social marketing reduces geographic and socioeconomic disparities and reaches men and boys.<sup id="cite_ref-AutoW0-19_34-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AutoW0-19-34">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Social franchising designs a brand for contraceptives in order to expand the market for contraceptives.<sup id="cite_ref-AutoW0-19_34-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AutoW0-19-34">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Drug shops and pharmacies provide health care in rural areas and urban slums where there are few public clinics. They account for most of the private sector provided contraception in sub-Saharan Africa, especially for condoms, pills, injectables and emergency contraception. Pharmacy supply and low-cost emergency contraception in South Africa and many low-income countries increased access to contraception.<sup id="cite_ref-AutoW0-19_34-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AutoW0-19-34">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Workplace policies and programs help expand access to family planning information. The Family Guidance Association of Ethiopia, which works with more than 150 enterprises to improve health services, analyzed health outcomes in one factory over 10 years and found reductions in unintended pregnancies and STIs as well as sick leave. Contraception use rose from 11% to 90% between 1997 and 2000. In 2016, the Bangladesh Garment Manufacturers Export Association partnered with family planning organizations to provide training and free contraceptives to factory clinics, creating the potential to reach thousands of factory employees.<sup id="cite_ref-AutoW0-19_34-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AutoW0-19-34">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="Non-governmental_organizations_.28NGOs.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Non-governmental_organizations_(NGOs)">Non-governmental organizations (NGOs)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Family_planning&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Non-governmental organizations (NGOs)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>NGOs may meet the needs of local poor by encouraging self-help and participation, understanding social and cultural subtleties, and working around red tape when governments do not adequately meet the needs of their constituents. A successful NGO can uphold family planning services even when a national program is threatened by political forces. NGOs can contribute to informing government policy, developing programs, or carry out programs that the government will not or can not implement.<sup id="cite_ref-pmid12283526_35-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pmid12283526-35">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="International_oversight">International oversight</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Family_planning&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: International oversight">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/International_Planned_Parenthood_Federation" title="International Planned Parenthood Federation">International Planned Parenthood Federation</a>, <a href="/wiki/Marie_Stopes_International" title="Marie Stopes International">Marie Stopes International</a>, and <a href="/wiki/United_States_Agency_for_International_Development" title="United States Agency for International Development">United States Agency for International Development</a></div>
<p>Family planning programs are now considered a key part of a comprehensive development strategy. The United Nations Millennium Development Goals (now superseded by the <a href="/wiki/Sustainable_Development_Goals" title="Sustainable Development Goals">Sustainable Development Goals</a>) reflects this international consensus. The 2012 London Summit on Family Planning, hosted by the UK government and the Bill and Melinda Gates Foundation, affirmed political commitments and increased funds for the project, strengthening the role of family planning in global development.<sup id="cite_ref-pmid24931078_36-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pmid24931078-36">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup>  Family Planning 2020 is the result of the 2012 London Summit on Family Planning where more than 20 governments made commitments to address the policy, financing, delivery, and socio-cultural barriers to women accessing contraception formation and services. FP2020 is a global movement that supports the rights of women to decide for themselves whether, when and how many children they want to have.<sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup> The commitments of the program are specific to each country, as compared to the generalized main goals of the 1995 conference program of action. FP2020 is hosted by the United Nations Foundation and operates in support of the UN Secretary-General’s Global Strategy for Women’s, Children’s and Adolescent’s Health.
</p><p>The world's largest international source of funding for population and reproductive health programs is the <a href="/wiki/United_Nations_Population_Fund" title="United Nations Population Fund">United Nations Population Fund</a> (UNFPA). In 1994, the <a href="/wiki/International_Conference_on_Population_and_Development" title="International Conference on Population and Development">International Conference on Population and Development</a> set the main goals of its Program of Action as:
</p>
<ul><li>Universal access to <a href="/wiki/Reproductive_health" title="Reproductive health">reproductive health</a> services by 2015</li>
<li>Universal primary education and ending the gender gap in education by 2015</li>
<li>Reducing maternal mortality by 75% by 2015</li>
<li>Reducing <a href="/wiki/Infant_mortality" title="Infant mortality">infant mortality</a></li>
<li>Increasing life expectancy at birth</li>
<li>Reducing <a href="/wiki/HIV" title="HIV">HIV</a> infection rates in persons aged 15–24 years by 25% in the most-affected countries by 2005, and by 25% globally by 2010</li></ul>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/World_Health_Organization" title="World Health Organization">World Health Organization</a> (WHO) and <a href="/wiki/World_Bank" title="World Bank">World Bank</a> estimate that $3 per person per year would provide basic family planning, maternal and neonatal health care to women in developing countries.  This would include <a href="/wiki/Contraception" class="mw-redirect" title="Contraception">contraception</a>, prenatal, delivery, and post-natal care in addition to postpartum family planning and the promotion of <a href="/wiki/Condoms" class="mw-redirect" title="Condoms">condoms</a> to prevent <a href="/wiki/Sexually_transmitted_infections" class="mw-redirect" title="Sexually transmitted infections">sexually transmitted infections</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Coercive_interference_with_family_planning">Coercive interference with family planning</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Family_planning&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Coercive interference with family planning">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Forced_sterilization">Forced sterilization</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Family_planning&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Forced sterilization">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Forced_sterilization" class="mw-redirect" title="Forced sterilization">Forced sterilization</a></div>
<p>Compulsory or forced sterilization programs or government policy attempt to force people to undergo surgical sterilization without their freely given consent. People from marginalized communities are at most risk of forced sterilization.<sup id="cite_ref-stopvaw.org_39-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-stopvaw.org-39">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup> Forced sterilization has occurred in recent years in Eastern Europe (against Roma women),<sup id="cite_ref-stopvaw.org_39-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-stopvaw.org-39">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup> and in Peru (during the 1990s against indigenous women).<sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/China%27s_one-child_policy" class="mw-redirect" title="China&#39;s one-child policy">China's one-child policy</a> was intended to limit the rise in population numbers, but in some situations involved forced sterilisation.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Sexual_violence">Sexual violence</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Family_planning&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Sexual violence">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Pregnancy_from_rape" title="Pregnancy from rape">Pregnancy from rape</a></div>
<p>Rape can result in a pregnancy. Rape can occur in a variety of situations, including <a href="/wiki/War_rape" class="mw-redirect" title="War rape">war rape</a>, <a href="/wiki/Forced_prostitution" title="Forced prostitution">forced prostitution</a> and <a href="/wiki/Marital_rape" title="Marital rape">marital rape</a>
</p><p>In <a href="/wiki/Rwanda" title="Rwanda">Rwanda</a>, the National Population Office has estimated that between 2,000 and 5,000 children were born as a result of sexual violence perpetrated during the <a href="/wiki/Rwandan_genocide" title="Rwandan genocide">genocide</a>, but victims' groups gave a higher estimated number of over 10,000 children.<sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span id="Family_planning.2C_human_rights.2C_and_development"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Family_planning,_human_rights,_and_development">Family planning, human rights, and development</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Family_planning&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Family planning, human rights, and development">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Access to safe, voluntary family planning is a human right and is central to gender equality, women’s empowerment and poverty reduction. The <a href="/wiki/United_Nations_Population_Fund" title="United Nations Population Fund">United Nations Population Fund</a> (UNFPA) says that, “Some 225 million women who want to avoid pregnancy are not using safe and effective family planning methods, for reasons ranging from lack access to information or services to lack of support from their partners or communities.” <sup id="cite_ref-unfpa2_43-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-unfpa2-43">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup> UNFPA says that, “Most of these women with an unmet need for contraceptives live in 69 of the poorest countries on earth.” <sup id="cite_ref-unfpa2_43-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-unfpa2-43">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Over the past 50 years, right-based family planning has enabled the cycle of poverty to be broken resulting in millions of women and children’s lives being saved.<sup id="cite_ref-unfpa2_43-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-unfpa2-43">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>
UNFPA says that, </p><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r856303468"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>Global consensus that family planning is a human right was secured at the 1994 International Conference on Population and Development, in Principle 8 of the Programme of Action: All couples and individuals have the basic right to decide freely and responsibly the number and spacing of their children and to have the information, education, and means to do so.<sup id="cite_ref-unfpa2_43-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-unfpa2-43">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup>
</p></blockquote>
<p>As part of the <a href="/wiki/United_Nations_Millennium_Development_Goals" class="mw-redirect" title="United Nations Millennium Development Goals">United Nations Millennium Development Goals</a> (MDGs) universal access to family planning is one of the key factors contributing to development and reducing poverty. Family planning creates benefits in areas such as, gender quality and women's health, access to sexual education and higher education, and improvements in maternal and child health.<sup id="cite_ref-unfpa2_43-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-unfpa2-43">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup> Note that the Millennium Development Goals have been superseded by the <a href="/wiki/Sustainable_Development_Goals" title="Sustainable Development Goals">Sustainable Development Goals</a>.
</p><p>UNFPA and the Guttmacher Institute say that,
</p>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r856303468"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>Serving all women in developing countries that currently have an unmet need for modern contraceptives would prevent an additional 54 million unintended pregnancies, including 21 million unplanned births, 26 million abortions and seven million miscarriages; this would also prevent 79,000 maternal deaths and 1.1 million infant deaths.<sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup>
</p></blockquote>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Quality-quantity_trade-off">Quality-quantity trade-off</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Family_planning&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Quality-quantity trade-off">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Having children produces a quality-quantity trade-off: parents need to decide how many children to have and how much to invest in the future of each child.<sup id="cite_ref-:0_45-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-45">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup> The increasing marginal cost of quality (child outcome) with respect to quantity (number of children) creates a trade-off between quantity and quality.<sup id="cite_ref-pmid18390301_46-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pmid18390301-46">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup> The quantity-quality trade-off means that policies that raise benefits of investing in child quality will generate higher levels of human capital, and policies that lower the costs of having children may have unintended adverse consequences on long-run economic growth. When deciding how many children, parents are influenced by their income level, perceived return to human capital investment, and cultural norms related to gender equality. Controlling birth rates allows families to raise the future earnings power of the next generation.
</p><p>Many empirical studies have tested the quantity-quality trade-off and either observed a negative correlation between family size and child quality or did not find a correlation.<sup id="cite_ref-pmid18390301_46-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pmid18390301-46">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup> Most studies treat family size as an exogenous variable because parents choose childbearing and child outcome and therefore cannot establish causality. They are both influenced by typically non-observable parental preferences and household characteristics, but some studies observe proxy variables such as investment in education.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (April 2018)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Developing_countries">Developing countries</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Family_planning&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Developing countries">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>High fertility countries have 18% of the world's population but contribute 38% of the population growth.<sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup> In order to become rich, resources must be re-appropriated to increase income per person rather than supporting larger populations. As populations increase, governments must accommodate increasing investments in health and human capital and institutional reforms to address demographic divides. Reducing the cost of human capital can be implemented by subsidizing education, which raises the earning power of women and the opportunity cost of having children, consequently lowering fertility.<sup id="cite_ref-:0_45-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-45">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup> Access to contraceptives may also yield lower fertility rates: having more children than expected constrains the individual from attaining their desired level of investment in child quantity and quality.<sup id="cite_ref-:0_45-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-45">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup>  In high fertility contexts, reduced fertility may contribute to economic development by improving child outcomes, reducing maternal mortality and increasing female human capital.
</p><p>Dang and Rogers (2015) show that in Vietnam, family planning services increased investment in education by lowering the relative cost of child quality and encouraging families to invest in quality.<sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup> By observing the distance to the nearest family planning center and the general education expenditure on each child, Dang and Rogers provide evidence that parents in Vietnam are making a child quality-quantity trade-off.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Demand_for_private_tutoring.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6a/Demand_for_private_tutoring.jpg/220px-Demand_for_private_tutoring.jpg" width="220" height="124" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6a/Demand_for_private_tutoring.jpg/330px-Demand_for_private_tutoring.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6a/Demand_for_private_tutoring.jpg/440px-Demand_for_private_tutoring.jpg 2x" data-file-width="960" data-file-height="540" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Demand_for_private_tutoring.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Demand for Private Tutoring with and without access to family planning</div></div></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Developed_countries">Developed countries</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Family_planning&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Developed countries">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Currently, developed countries have experienced rising economic growth and falling fertility. As a result of the demographic transition that takes place when countries become rich, developed countries have an increasing proportion of retired people which raises the burden on the workforce population to support pensions and social programs. Encouraging higher fertility as a solution may risk reversing the benefits for increased child investment and female labor force participation have had on economic growth. Increasing high skill migration may be an effective way to increase the return to education leading to lower fertility and a greater supply of highly skilled individuals.<sup id="cite_ref-:0_45-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-45">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Demand_for_family_planning">Demand for family planning</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Family_planning&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Demand for family planning">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>214 million women of reproductive age in developing countries who do not want to become pregnant are not using a modern contraceptive method.<sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup> This could be a result of a limited choice of methods, limited access to contraception, fear of side-effects, cultural or religious opposition, poor quality of available services, user or provider bias, or gender-based barriers. In Africa, 24.2% of women of reproductive age do not have access to modern contraction. In Asia, Latin America, and the Caribbean, the unmet need is 10–11%. Meeting the unmet need for contraception could prevent 104,000 maternal deaths per year, a 29% reduction of women dying from postpartum hemorrhage or unsafe abortions.<sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>According to the United Nations Department of Economic and Social Affairs: Population Division, 64% of the world uses contraceptives, 12% of the world population's need for contraceptives is unmet. In the least developed countries, 22% of the population do not have access to contraceptives, and 40% use contraceptives.<sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup> The unmet need for modern contraceptives is very high in sub-Saharan Africa, south Asia, and western Asia. Africa has the lowest rate of contraceptive use (33%) and highest rate of unmet need (22%). Northern America has the highest rate of contraceptive use (73%) and the lowest unmet need (7%). Latin America and the Caribbean follows closely behind with 73% contraceptive use and 11% unmet need. Europe and Asia are on par: Europe has a 69% contraceptive use rate and 10% unmet need, Asia has a 68% contraceptive use and 10% unmet need. Although unmet need is lower in Asia because of the large population in this region, the number of women with unmet need is 443 million, compared to 74 million in Europe  Oceania has a 59% contraceptive use rate and 15% unmet need. When comparing the regions within these continents, Eastern Asia ranks the highest rate of contraceptive use (82%) and lowest unmet need (5%). Western Africa ranks the lowest rate of contraceptive use (17%). Middle Africa ranks the highest unmet need (26%). Unmet need is higher among poorer women; in Bolivia and Ethiopa unmet need is tripled and doubled among poor populations.<sup id="cite_ref-prb.org_52-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-prb.org-52">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup> However, in the Democratic Republic of Congo and Liberia the rates of unmet need are different by 1–2 percentage points.<sup id="cite_ref-prb.org_52-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-prb.org-52">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup> This suggests that as wealthier women begin to want smaller families, they will increasingly seek out family planning methods.<sup id="cite_ref-prb.org_52-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-prb.org-52">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Contraceptive_prevalence_and_unmet_need_for_family_planning_among_married_or_in-union_women_aged_15_to_49_years,_2015.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/14/Contraceptive_prevalence_and_unmet_need_for_family_planning_among_married_or_in-union_women_aged_15_to_49_years%2C_2015.jpg/220px-Contraceptive_prevalence_and_unmet_need_for_family_planning_among_married_or_in-union_women_aged_15_to_49_years%2C_2015.jpg" width="220" height="285" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/14/Contraceptive_prevalence_and_unmet_need_for_family_planning_among_married_or_in-union_women_aged_15_to_49_years%2C_2015.jpg/330px-Contraceptive_prevalence_and_unmet_need_for_family_planning_among_married_or_in-union_women_aged_15_to_49_years%2C_2015.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/14/Contraceptive_prevalence_and_unmet_need_for_family_planning_among_married_or_in-union_women_aged_15_to_49_years%2C_2015.jpg/440px-Contraceptive_prevalence_and_unmet_need_for_family_planning_among_married_or_in-union_women_aged_15_to_49_years%2C_2015.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1275" data-file-height="1650" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Contraceptive_prevalence_and_unmet_need_for_family_planning_among_married_or_in-union_women_aged_15_to_49_years,_2015.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>United Nations Department of Economic and Social Affairs, Population Division, "Trends in Contraceptive Use Worldwide 2015" New York: United Nations, 2015.</div></div></div>
<p>Substantial unmet need has provoked family planning programs by governments and donors, but the impact of family planning programs on fertility and contraceptive use remains somewhat unsettled. "Demand theory" argues that in traditional agricultural societies, fertility rates are driven by the desire to offset high mortality, thus as society modernizes, the costs of raising children increases, reducing their economic value, and resulting in a decline in desired number of children. Under this theory, family planning programs will have a marginal impact. Bongaarts (2014) shows that using a country case study approach, both stronger and weaker family programs reduce the unmet need for contraceptives and increases use by making modern contraceptives more widely available and removing obstacles to use.<sup id="cite_ref-pmid24931078_36-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pmid24931078-36">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup> Also, the demand that is satisfied and the proportion of women using modern methods increased. The programs may have an additional effect of diffusing the ideas related to family planning and thus raising the demand for contraception. As a result, a small decrease in unmet need may be offset by a rise in demand. Nonetheless, even in countries where it is assumed that family programs will make a marginal impact, Bongaarts shows that family planning programs can potentially increase contraceptive use and increase/decrease demand depending on the preexisting attitudes of the community.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Regional_variations">Regional variations</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Family_planning&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Regional variations">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Familyplanningmalaysia.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6e/Familyplanningmalaysia.jpg/220px-Familyplanningmalaysia.jpg" width="220" height="337" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6e/Familyplanningmalaysia.jpg/330px-Familyplanningmalaysia.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/6/6e/Familyplanningmalaysia.jpg 2x" data-file-width="392" data-file-height="600" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Familyplanningmalaysia.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A family planning facility in Kuala Terengganu, <a href="/wiki/Malaysia" title="Malaysia">Malaysia</a></div></div></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Africa">Africa</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Family_planning&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Africa">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Birth_control_in_Africa" title="Birth control in Africa">Birth control in Africa</a></div>
<p>Most of the countries with lowest rates of contraceptive use, highest maternal, infant, and child mortality rates, and highest fertility rates are in Africa.<sup id="cite_ref-birth_rate_53-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-birth_rate-53">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Contraceptive_prevalence_54-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Contraceptive_prevalence-54">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Maternal_mortality_ratio_55-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Maternal_mortality_ratio-55">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Fertility_rate_56-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Fertility_rate-56">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Mortality_rate,_under-5_57-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mortality_rate,_under-5-57">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup>
Only about 30% of all women use birth control, although over half of all African women would like to use birth control if it was available to them.<sup id="cite_ref-Familyplanning_58-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Familyplanning-58">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Cleland_59-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cleland-59">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup> The main problems that preventing access to and use of birth control are unavailability, poor health care services, spousal disapproval, religious concerns, and misinformation about the effects of birth control.<sup id="cite_ref-Familyplanning_58-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Familyplanning-58">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup>  The most available type of birth control is condoms.<sup id="cite_ref-guttmacher_60-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-guttmacher-60">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup>
A rapidly growing population coupled with an increase in preventable diseases means countries in Sub-Saharan Africa face an increasingly younger population.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="China">China</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Family_planning&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: China">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<table class="plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-content ambox-Update" role="presentation"><tbody><tr><td class="mbox-image"><div style="width:52px"><img alt="Ambox current red.svg" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/Ambox_current_red.svg/42px-Ambox_current_red.svg.png" width="42" height="34" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/Ambox_current_red.svg/63px-Ambox_current_red.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/Ambox_current_red.svg/84px-Ambox_current_red.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="360" data-file-height="290" /></div></td><td class="mbox-text"><div class="mbox-text-span">This article needs to be <b>updated</b>.<span class="hide-when-compact"> Please update this article to reflect recent events or newly available information.</span>  <small><i>(June 2016)</i></small></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Family_planning_policy" title="Family planning policy">Family planning policy</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/People%27s_Republic_of_China" class="mw-redirect" title="People&#39;s Republic of China">China</a>'s <i>Family planning policy</i> forced couples to have no more than <a href="/wiki/One-child_policy" title="One-child policy">one child</a>. Beginning in 1979 and being officially phased out in 2015,<sup id="cite_ref-pmid10514169_61-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pmid10514169-61">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup> the policy was instated to control the rapid population growth that was occurring in the nation at that time. With the rapid change in population, China was facing many impacts, including poverty and homelessness. As a developing nation, the Chinese government was concerned that a continuation of the rapid population growth that had been occurring would hinder their development as a nation. The process of family planning varied throughout China, as people differed in their responsiveness to the one-child policy, based on location and socioeconomic status. For example, many families in the cities accepted the policy more readily based on the lack of space, money, and resources that often occurs in the cities. Another example can be found in the enforcement of this rule; people living in rural areas of China were, in some cases, permitted to have more than one child, but had to wait several years after the birth of the first one.<sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup>  However, the people in rural areas of China were more hesitant in accepting this policy. China's population policy has been credited with a very significant slowing of China's population growth which had been higher before the policy was implemented. However, the policy has come under criticism that it has resulted in the abuse of women. Often implementation of the policy has involved forced abortions, <a href="/wiki/Forced_sterilization" class="mw-redirect" title="Forced sterilization">forced sterilization</a>, and infanticides. That families desired a male child had a part to play in the number of infanticides. The number of girls that die within their first year of birth is twice that of boys.<sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup> Another drawback of the policy is that China’s elderly population is now increasing rapidly.<sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup> However, while the punishment of "unplanned" pregnancy is a large fine, both forced abortion and forced sterilization can be charged with intentional assault, which is punished with up to ten years' imprisonment.
Another issue that is raised in the one-child policy in China is the information in regards to naturally giving birth to twins or triplets. If this situation arises, the family is allowed to keep the children because of the natural causes of this impregnation.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (July 2014)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>
</p><p>Family planning in China had its benefits, and its drawbacks. For example, it helped reduce the population by about 300 million people in its first 20 years.<sup id="cite_ref-FlorCruz_65-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FlorCruz-65">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup> A drawback is that there are now millions of sibling-less people, and in China siblings are very important. Once the parent generation gets older, the children help take care of them, and the work is usually equally split among the siblings.<sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup> Another benefit of the implementation of the one-child law is that it reduced the fertility rate from about  2.75 children born per woman, to about 1.8 children born per woman in the 1979.<sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Hong_Kong">Hong Kong</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Family_planning&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Hong Kong">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/The_Family_Planning_Association_of_Hong_Kong" title="The Family Planning Association of Hong Kong">The Family Planning Association of Hong Kong</a></div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Two-child_policy" title="Two-child policy">Two-child policy</a></div>
<p>In Hong Kong, the Eugenics League was found in 1936, which became <a href="/wiki/The_Family_Planning_Association_of_Hong_Kong" title="The Family Planning Association of Hong Kong">The Family Planning Association of Hong Kong</a> in 1950.<sup id="cite_ref-famplan.org.hk_68-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-famplan.org.hk-68">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup> The organisation provides family planning advice, <a href="/wiki/Sex_education" title="Sex education">sex education</a>, birth control services to the general public of <a href="/wiki/Hong_Kong" title="Hong Kong">Hong Kong</a>. In the 1970s, due to the rapidly rising population, it launched the "Two Is Enough" campaign, which reduced the general birth rate through educational means.<sup id="cite_ref-famplan.org.hk_68-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-famplan.org.hk-68">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/The_Family_Planning_Association_of_Hong_Kong" title="The Family Planning Association of Hong Kong">The Family Planning Association of Hong Kong</a>, Hong Kong's national family planning association,<sup id="cite_ref-ippf.org_69-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ippf.org-69">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup>  founded the <a href="/wiki/International_Planned_Parenthood_Federation" title="International Planned Parenthood Federation">International Planned Parenthood Federation</a> with its counterparts in seven other countries.<sup id="cite_ref-ippf.org_69-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ippf.org-69">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="India">India</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Family_planning&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: India">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Family_planning_in_India" title="Family planning in India">Family planning in India</a></div>
<p>Family planning in <a href="/wiki/India" title="India">India</a> is based on efforts largely sponsored by the Indian <a href="/wiki/Government_of_India" title="Government of India">government</a>. In the 1965–2009 period, contraceptive usage has more than tripled (from 13% of married women in 1970 to 48% in 2009) and the fertility rate has more than halved (from 5.7 in 1966 to 2.6 in 2009), but the national fertility rate is still high enough to cause long-term population growth. India adds up to 1,000,000 people to its population every 15 days.<sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-rengel2000_71-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-rengel2000-71">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-who2009gdj_72-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-who2009gdj-72">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-ramu2006_73-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ramu2006-73">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-bucen1997ahs_74-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bucen1997ahs-74">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Iran">Iran</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Family_planning&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Iran">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Family_planning_in_Iran" title="Family planning in Iran">Family planning in Iran</a></div>
<p>While Iran's population grew at a rate of more than 3% per year between 1956 and 1986, the growth rate began to decline in the late 1980s and early 1990s after the government initiated a major population control program. By 2007 the growth rate had declined to 0.7 percent per year, with a birth rate of 17 per 1,000 persons and a death rate of 6 per 1,000.<sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup> Reports by the UN show birth control policies in Iran to be effective with the country topping the list of greatest fertility decreases. UN's Population Division of the Department of Economic and Social Affairs says that between 1975 and 1980, the total fertility number was 6.5. The projected level for Iran's 2005 to 2010 birth rate is fewer than two.<sup id="cite_ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In late July 2012, <a href="/wiki/Supreme_Leader" class="mw-redirect" title="Supreme Leader">Supreme Leader</a> <a href="/wiki/Ali_Khamenei" title="Ali Khamenei">Ali Khamenei</a> described Iran's contraceptive services as "wrong," and Iranian authorities are slashing birth-control programs in what one Western newspaper (<a href="/wiki/USA_Today" title="USA Today">USA Today</a>) describes as a "major reversal" of its long standing policy. Whether program cuts and high-level appeals for bigger families will be successful is still unclear.<sup id="cite_ref-boom_77-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-boom-77">&#91;77&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Ireland">Ireland</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Family_planning&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Ireland">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Contraception_in_the_Republic_of_Ireland" title="Contraception in the Republic of Ireland">Contraception in the Republic of Ireland</a></div>
<p>The sale of contraceptives was illegal in Ireland from 1935 until 1980, when it was legalized with strong restrictions, later loosened.
