It has been argued that the resulting <a href="/wiki/Demographic_dividend" title="Demographic dividend">demographic dividend</a> played a role in the economic boom in Ireland that began in the 1990s and ended abruptly in 2008 (the <a href="/wiki/Celtic_tiger" class="mw-redirect" title="Celtic tiger">Celtic tiger</a>) was in part due to the <a href="/wiki/An_Irish_solution_to_an_Irish_problem" title="An Irish solution to an Irish problem">legalisation of contraception in 1979</a> and subsequent decline in the fertility rate.<sup id="cite_ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78">&#91;78&#93;</a></sup> In Ireland the ratio of workers to dependents increased due to lower fertility — the reality of which has been questioned<sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79">&#91;79&#93;</a></sup> — but was raised further by increased female labor market participation.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (April 2012)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Pakistan">Pakistan</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Family_planning&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Pakistan">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Family_planning_in_Pakistan" title="Family planning in Pakistan">Family planning in Pakistan</a></div>
<p>In agreement with the 1994 <a href="/wiki/International_Conference_on_Population_and_Development" title="International Conference on Population and Development">International Conference on Population and Development</a> in Cairo, <a href="/wiki/Pakistan" title="Pakistan">Pakistan</a> pledged that by 2010 it would provide universal access to family planning. Additionally, Pakistan’s Poverty Reduction Strategy Paper has set specific national goals for increases in family planning and <a href="/wiki/Contraceptive" class="mw-redirect" title="Contraceptive">contraceptive</a> use.<sup id="cite_ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup>  In 2011 just one in five Pakistani women ages 15 to 49 uses modern birth control.<sup id="cite_ref-Brulliard_81-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brulliard-81">&#91;81&#93;</a></sup> Contraception is shunned under traditional social mores that are fiercely defended as fundamentalist Islam gains strength.<sup id="cite_ref-Brulliard_81-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brulliard-81">&#91;81&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Russia">Russia</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Family_planning&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: Russia">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Demographics_of_Russia" title="Demographics of Russia">Demographics of Russia</a></div>
<p>According to a 2004 study, current pregnancies were termed "desired and timely" by 58% of respondents, while 23% described them as "desired, but untimely", and 19% said they were "undesired".  As of 2004, the share of women of reproductive age using hormonal or intrauterine birth control methods was about 46% (29% intrauterine, 17% hormonal).<sup id="cite_ref-und_82-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-und-82">&#91;82&#93;</a></sup>  During the soviet era high quality contraceptives were difficult to obtain, and abortion became the most common way of preventing unwanted births. Since the dissolution of the Soviet Union abortion rates have fallen considerably, but they are still higher than rates in many developed countries.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Philippines">Philippines</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Family_planning&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: Philippines">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In the <a href="/wiki/Philippines" title="Philippines">Philippines</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Responsible_Parenthood_and_Reproductive_Health_Act_of_2012" title="Responsible Parenthood and Reproductive Health Act of 2012">Responsible Parenthood and Reproductive Health Act of 2012</a> guarantees universal access to methods on <a href="/wiki/Contraception" class="mw-redirect" title="Contraception">contraception</a>, fertility control, <a href="/wiki/Sexual_education" class="mw-redirect" title="Sexual education">sexual education</a>, and maternal care. While there is general agreement about its provisions on maternal and child health, there is great debate on its mandate that the Philippine government and the private sector will fund and undertake widespread distribution of family planning devices such as <a href="/wiki/Condoms" class="mw-redirect" title="Condoms">condoms</a>, <a href="/wiki/Birth_control_pill" class="mw-redirect" title="Birth control pill">birth control pills</a>, and <a href="/wiki/IUD" class="mw-redirect" title="IUD">IUDs</a>, as the government continues to disseminate information on their use through all health care centers.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Thailand">Thailand</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Family_planning&amp;action=edit&amp;section=33" title="Edit section: Thailand">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In 1970, Thailand's government declared a population policy that would battle the country's rapid population growth rate. This policy set a 5-year goal to reduce Thailand's population growth rate from 3 percent to 2.5 percent through methods such as spreading family planning awareness to rural families, or integrating family planning activities into maternal and child healthcare education.<sup id="cite_ref-83" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-83">&#91;83&#93;</a></sup> Public figures such as <a href="/wiki/Mechai_Viravaidya" title="Mechai Viravaidya">Mechai Viravaidya</a> helped spread family planning awareness through public speakings and charitable activities.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Singapore">Singapore</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Family_planning&amp;action=edit&amp;section=34" title="Edit section: Singapore">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Family_planning_in_Singapore" class="mw-redirect" title="Family planning in Singapore">Family planning in Singapore</a></div>
<p>Population control in Singapore spans two distinct phases: first to slow and reverse the boom in births that started after <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a>; and then, from the 1980s onwards, to encourage parents to have more children because birth numbers had fallen below <a href="/wiki/Sub-replacement_fertility" title="Sub-replacement fertility">replacement levels</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="United_Kingdom">United Kingdom</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Family_planning&amp;action=edit&amp;section=35" title="Edit section: United Kingdom">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Contraception has been available for free under the <a href="/wiki/National_Health_Service" title="National Health Service">National Health Service</a> since 1974, and 74% of reproductive-age women use some form of contraception.<sup id="cite_ref-pmid17911129_84-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pmid17911129-84">&#91;84&#93;</a></sup> The levonorgestrel <a href="/wiki/Intrauterine_system" class="mw-redirect" title="Intrauterine system">intrauterine system</a> has been massively popular.<sup id="cite_ref-pmid17911129_84-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pmid17911129-84">&#91;84&#93;</a></sup> Sterilization is popular in older age groups, among those 45–49, 29% of men and 21% of women have been sterilized.<sup id="cite_ref-pmid17911129_84-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pmid17911129-84">&#91;84&#93;</a></sup> Female sterilization has been declining since 1996, when the intrauterine system was introduced.<sup id="cite_ref-pmid17911129_84-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pmid17911129-84">&#91;84&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Emergency_contraception" title="Emergency contraception">Emergency contraception</a> has been available since the 1970s, a product was specifically licensed for emergency contraception in 1984, and emergency contraceptives became available over the counter in 2001.<sup id="cite_ref-pmid17911129_84-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pmid17911129-84">&#91;84&#93;</a></sup> Since becoming available over the counter it has not reduced the use of other forms of contraception, as some moralists feared it might.<sup id="cite_ref-pmid17911129_84-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pmid17911129-84">&#91;84&#93;</a></sup> In any year only 5% of women of childbearing age use emergency hormonal contraception.<sup id="cite_ref-pmid17911129_84-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pmid17911129-84">&#91;84&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Despite widespread availability of contraceptives, almost half of pregnancies were unintended in 2005.<sup id="cite_ref-pmid17911129_84-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pmid17911129-84">&#91;84&#93;</a></sup>  <a href="/wiki/Abortion_in_the_United_Kingdom" title="Abortion in the United Kingdom">Abortion</a> was legalized in 1967.<sup id="cite_ref-pmid17911129_84-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pmid17911129-84">&#91;84&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="United_States">United States</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Family_planning&amp;action=edit&amp;section=36" title="Edit section: United States">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Birth_control_in_the_United_States" title="Birth control in the United States">Birth control in the United States</a></div>
<p>Despite the availability of highly effective contraceptives, about half of U.S. <a href="/wiki/Unintended_pregnancy" title="Unintended pregnancy">pregnancies are unintended</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-pmid19041435_30-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pmid19041435-30">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup> Highly effective contraceptives, such as <a href="/wiki/IUD" class="mw-redirect" title="IUD">IUD</a>, are underused in the United States.<sup id="cite_ref-Cleland_59-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cleland-59">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup> Increasing use of highly effective contraceptives could help meet the goal set forward in <a href="/wiki/Healthy_people" class="mw-redirect" title="Healthy people">Healthy People 2020</a> to decrease unintended pregnancy by 10%.<sup id="cite_ref-Cleland_59-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cleland-59">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup>  Cost to the user is one factor preventing many American women from using more effective contraceptives.<sup id="cite_ref-Cleland_59-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cleland-59">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup>  Making contraceptives available without a copay increases use of highly effective methods, reduces unintended pregnancies, and may be instrumental in achieving the Healthy People 2020 goal.<sup id="cite_ref-Cleland_59-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cleland-59">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In the United States, contraceptive use saves about $19 billion in direct medical costs each year.<sup id="cite_ref-pmid19041435_30-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pmid19041435-30">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Title_X" title="Title X">Title&#160;X</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Public_Health_Service_Act" title="Public Health Service Act">Public Health Service Act</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-85" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-85">&#91;85&#93;</a></sup> is a U.S. government program dedicated to providing family planning services for those in need.  But funding for Title&#160;X as a percentage of total public funding to family planning client services has steadily declined from 44% of total expenditures in 1980 to 12% in 2006. Current funding for Title X is less than 40% of what is needed to meet the need for publicly funded family planning.<sup id="cite_ref-nationalfamilyplanning.org_86-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nationalfamilyplanning.org-86">&#91;86&#93;</a></sup> Title X would need $737 million annually to meet the need for family planning services.<sup id="cite_ref-nationalfamilyplanning.org_86-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nationalfamilyplanning.org-86">&#91;86&#93;</a></sup> Only 6.2 million women accessed publicly funded services from 10,700 clinics in 2015, despite an estimated 20 million women who could benefit.
</p><p>Clinics funded by Title X served 3.8 million of these women with access to services.In 2015, publicly funded contraceptive services helped women prevent 1.9 million unintended pregnancies; 876,100 of these would have resulted in unplanned births and 628,000 abortions.<sup id="cite_ref-auto_87-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-auto-87">&#91;87&#93;</a></sup> Without publicly funded contraceptive services, the rates of unintended pregnancies, unplanned births and abortions would have been 67% higher.<sup id="cite_ref-auto_87-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-auto-87">&#91;87&#93;</a></sup> The rates for teens would have been 102% higher.<sup id="cite_ref-auto_87-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-auto-87">&#91;87&#93;</a></sup> Title X funded programs saw 1.2 million fewer patients in 2015 compared to 2010 as funding decreased by $31 million.<sup id="cite_ref-auto_87-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-auto-87">&#91;87&#93;</a></sup> In 2015, an estimated 2.4 million additional women received Medicaid-funded contraceptive services from private doctors.<sup id="cite_ref-88" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-88">&#91;88&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Medicaid" title="Medicaid">Medicaid</a> has increased from 20% to 71% from 1980 to 2006. In 2006, Medicaid contributed $1.3 billion to public family planning.<sup id="cite_ref-89" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-89">&#91;89&#93;</a></sup>  The $1.9 billion spent on publicly funded family planning in 2008 saved an estimated $7 billion in short-term Medicaid costs.<sup id="cite_ref-pmid21506736_90-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pmid21506736-90">&#91;90&#93;</a></sup>  Such services helped women prevent an estimated 1.94 million unintended pregnancies and 810,000 abortions.<sup id="cite_ref-pmid21506736_90-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pmid21506736-90">&#91;90&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>About 3 out of 10 women in the United States have an abortion by the time they are 45 years old.<sup id="cite_ref-91" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-91">&#91;91&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>A 2017 paper found that parents' access to family planning programs had a positive economic impact on their subsequent children: " Using the county-level introduction of U.S. family planning programs between 1964 and 1973, we find that children born after programs began had 2.8% higher household incomes. They were also 7% less likely to live in poverty and 12% less likely to live in households receiving public assistance. After accounting for selection, the direct effects of family planning programs on parents’ incomes account for roughly two thirds of these gains."<sup id="cite_ref-92" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-92">&#91;92&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Uzbekistan">Uzbekistan</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Family_planning&amp;action=edit&amp;section=37" title="Edit section: Uzbekistan">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Compulsory_sterilization#Uzbekistan" title="Compulsory sterilization">Compulsory sterilization in Uzbekistan</a></div>
<p>In <a href="/wiki/Uzbekistan" title="Uzbekistan">Uzbekistan</a> the government has pushed for uteruses to be removed from women in order to forcibly sterilize them.<sup id="cite_ref-93" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-93">&#91;93&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-94" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-94">&#91;94&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Obstacles_to_family_planning">Obstacles to family planning</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Family_planning&amp;action=edit&amp;section=38" title="Edit section: Obstacles to family planning">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>There are many reasons as to why women do not use contraceptives. These reasons include logistical problems, scientific and religious concerns, limited access to transportation in order to access health clinics, lack of education and knowledge and opposition by partners, families or communities plus the fact that no one is able to control their fertility beyond basic behavior involving conception.
</p><p>UNFPA says that “efforts to increase access must be sensitive to cultural and national contexts, and must consider economic, geographic and age disparities within countries.” <sup id="cite_ref-unfpa1_19-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-unfpa1-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>UNFPA states that, “Poorer women and those in rural areas often have less access to family planning services. Certain groups — including adolescents, unmarried people, the urban poor, rural populations, sex workers and people living with HIV  also face a variety of barriers to family planning. This can lead to higher rates of unintended pregnancy, increased risk of HIV and other STIs, limited choice of contraceptive methods, and higher levels of unmet need for family planning.” <sup id="cite_ref-unfpa1_19-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-unfpa1-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>For national, international, or local health programs involved in family planning, the use of standard indicators<sup id="cite_ref-95" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-95">&#91;95&#93;</a></sup> is increasingly encouraged, to track barriers to effective family planning along with the efficacy, uptake, and provision of family planning services.<sup id="cite_ref-96" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-96">&#91;96&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="World_Contraception_Day">World Contraception Day</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Family_planning&amp;action=edit&amp;section=39" title="Edit section: World Contraception Day">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>September 26 is designated as World Contraception Day, devoted to raising awareness of contraception and improving education about sexual and reproductive health, with a vision of "a world where every pregnancy is wanted".<sup id="cite_ref-WorldContraceptionDay_97-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WorldContraceptionDay-97">&#91;97&#93;</a></sup>  It is supported by a group of international NGOs, including:
</p><p>Asian Pacific Council on Contraception, Centro Latinamericano Salud y Mujer, European Society of Contraception and Reproductive Health, <a href="/wiki/German_Foundation_for_World_Population" title="German Foundation for World Population">German Foundation for World Population</a>, International Federation of Pediatric and Adolescent Gynecology, <a href="/wiki/International_Planned_Parenthood_Federation" title="International Planned Parenthood Federation">International Planned Parenthood Federation</a>, <a href="/wiki/Marie_Stopes_International" title="Marie Stopes International">Marie Stopes International</a>, <a href="/wiki/Population_Services_International" title="Population Services International">Population Services International</a>, The <a href="/wiki/Population_Council" title="Population Council">Population Council</a>, The <abbr title="United States Agency for International Development"><a href="/wiki/United_States_Agency_for_International_Development" title="United States Agency for International Development">USAID</a></abbr>, <a href="/wiki/Women_Deliver" title="Women Deliver">Women Deliver</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-WorldContraceptionDay_97-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WorldContraceptionDay-97">&#91;97&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Abortion">Abortion</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Family_planning&amp;action=edit&amp;section=40" title="Edit section: Abortion">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Some pro-life groups claim that the United Nations and World Health Organization advocate abortion as a form of family planning.<sup id="cite_ref-98" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-98">&#91;98&#93;</a></sup> In fact, the United Nations Population Fund explicitly states it “never promotes abortion as a form of family planning.”<sup id="cite_ref-Family_planning_4-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Family_planning-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup> The World Health Organization states that "Family planning/contraception reduces the need for abortion, especially unsafe abortion."<sup id="cite_ref-Family_planning/Contraception_16-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Family_planning/Contraception-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The campaign to conflate contraception and abortion is rooted on the assertion that contraception ends, rather than prevents, pregnancy. According to an amicus brief submitted to the U.S. Supreme Court in October 2013 led by Physicians for Reproductive Health and the American College of Obstetricians and Gynecologists, a contraceptive method prevents pregnancy by interfering with fertilization, or implantation. Abortion, separate from contraceptives, ends an established pregnancy.<sup id="cite_ref-99" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-99">&#91;99&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
